           <ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha; margin-left: 1em;"> 
            <li value="1">The population of Bhutan had been estimated based on the reported figure of about 1 million in the 1970s when the country had joined the United Nations and precise statistics were lacking.<big><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></big> Thus, using the annual increase rate of 2–3%, the most population estimates were around 2 million in 2000. A national census was carried out in 2005 and it turned out that the population was 672,425. Consequently, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Division" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Population Division">United Nations Population Division</a> reduced its estimation of the country's population in the 2006 revision<big><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></big> for the whole period from 1950 to 2000.</li> 
           </ol>

      <p><b>Bhutan</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-Bhutan-pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/En-Bhutan-pronunciation.ogg" class="internal" title="En-Bhutan-pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Dzongkha_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Dzongkha language">Dzongkha</a>: <span lang="dz">འབྲུག་ཡུལ་</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Dzongkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanization of Dzongkha">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small><i lang="dz-Latn" title="Dzongkha-language romanization"><a href="/wiki/Druk" title="Druk">Druk</a> Yul</i>, <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ʈuk̚˩.yː˩]</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali:</a> भूटान), officially known as the <b>Kingdom of Bhutan</b> (<a href="/wiki/Dzongkha_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Dzongkha language">Dzongkha</a>: <span lang="dz">འབྲུག་རྒྱལ་ཁབ་</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Dzongkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanization of Dzongkha">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small><i lang="dz-Latn" title="Dzongkha-language romanization">Druk Gyal Khap</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Nepali: भूटान अधिराज्य'),<sup id="cite_ref-Driem478_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Driem478-11">[11]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Himalaya" title="Eastern Himalaya">Eastern Himalayas</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> to the south. <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> are located in proximity to Bhutan but do not share a land border. The country has a population of over 754,000<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and a territory of 38,394 square kilometers (14,824 sq mi) which ranks <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">133rd</a> in terms of land area, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population" title="List of countries and dependencies by population">160th</a> in population. Bhutan is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Vajrayana Buddhism">Vajrayana Buddhism</a> as the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Subalpine" class="mw-redirect" title="Subalpine">subalpine</a> Himalayan mountains in the north rise from the country's lush subtropical plains in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-Bhutan_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhutan-13">[13]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_of_Bhutan" title="Mountains of Bhutan">Bhutanese Himalayas</a>, there are peaks higher than <span class="nowrap">7,000 meters (23,000&nbsp;ft)</span> above <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gangkhar_Puensum" title="Gangkhar Puensum">Gangkhar Puensum</a> is Bhutan's highest peak and may also be the highest unclimbed mountain in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Itami2001_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Itami2001-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Bhutan" title="Wildlife of Bhutan">wildlife of Bhutan</a> is notable for its diversity,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> including the Himalayan <a href="/wiki/Takin" title="Takin">takin</a>. The largest city in Bhutan is the capital <a href="/wiki/Thimphu" title="Thimphu">Thimphu</a>. </p>
      <p>Bhutan and neighboring <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> experienced the <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Spread of Buddhism">spread of Buddhism</a> which originated in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> during the lifetime of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>. In the first millennium, the <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana</a> school of Buddhism spread to Bhutan from the southern <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Empire</a> of Bengal. Tibet, Bhutan, <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a> and parts of Nepal became the vestiges of the Mahayana schools amid the <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_Buddhism_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of Buddhism in India">decline of Buddhism in India</a>. Bhutan also came under the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/16th_century" title="16th century">16th-century</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ngawang_Namgyal" title="Ngawang Namgyal">Ngawang Namgyal</a> unified the <a href="/wiki/Valleys_of_Bhutan" title="Valleys of Bhutan">valleys of Bhutan</a> into a single state. Namgyal defeated three Tibetan invasions, subjugated rival religious schools, codified the <i><a href="/wiki/Tsa_Yig" title="Tsa Yig">Tsa Yig</a></i> legal system, and established a government of theocratic and civil administrators. Namgyal became the first <a href="/wiki/Zhabdrung_Rinpoche" title="Zhabdrung Rinpoche">Zhabdrung Rinpoche</a> and his successors acted as the spiritual leaders of Bhutan like the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> in Tibet. During the 17th century, Bhutan controlled large parts of <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">northeast India</a>, Sikkim and Nepal; it also wielded significant influence in <a href="/wiki/Cooch_Behar_State" title="Cooch Behar State">Cooch Behar State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Bhutan ceded the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Duars" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengal Duars">Bengal Duars</a> to <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Bhutan_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhutan War">Bhutan War</a> in the 19th century. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wangchuck" title="House of Wangchuck">House of Wangchuck</a> emerged as the monarchy and pursued closer ties with the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> in the subcontinent. In 1910, a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Punakha" title="Treaty of Punakha">treaty</a> guaranteed British advice in <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Bhutan" title="Foreign relations of Bhutan">foreign policy</a> in exchange for internal autonomy in Bhutan. The arrangement continued under a new treaty with India in 1949 in which both countries recognized each other's sovereignty. Bhutan joined the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1971. It has since expanded relations with 55 countries, including <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. While dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Military_Training_Team" title="Indian Military Training Team">Indian military</a>, Bhutan maintains its own <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bhutan_Army" title="Royal Bhutan Army">military units</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Bhutan" title="Constitution of Bhutan">2008 Constitution</a> establishes a parliamentary government with an elected <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Bhutan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of Bhutan">National Assembly</a> and a <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Bhutan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Council of Bhutan">National Council</a>. Bhutan is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation" title="South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation">South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation</a> (SAARC). In 2020, Bhutan ranked third in South Asia after <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Bhutan is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Climate_Vulnerable_Forum" title="Climate Vulnerable Forum">Climate Vulnerable Forum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/BIMSTEC" class="mw-redirect" title="BIMSTEC">BIMSTEC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO). Bhutan ranked first in SAARC in <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">economic freedom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ease_of_doing_business_index" title="Ease of doing business index">ease of doing business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">peace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">lack of corruption</a> in 2016. Bhutan has one of the largest water reserves for <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Melting_glacier" class="mw-redirect" title="Melting glacier">Melting glaciers</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> are a growing concern in Bhutan.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhutan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The precise etymology of "Bhutan" is unknown, although it is likely to derive from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Tibetan" title="Old Tibetan">Tibetan</a> <a href="/wiki/Endonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Endonym">endonym</a> "Böd" for <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>. Traditionally, it is taken to be a transcription of the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>Bhoṭa-anta</i> "end of <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>", a reference to Bhutan's position as the southern extremity of the Tibetan plateau and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Names&amp;Histories_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Names&amp;Histories-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lcweb2.loc.gov_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lcweb2.loc.gov-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since the 17th century, Bhutan's official name has been <i>Druk yul</i> (literally, "country of the <a href="/wiki/Drukpa_Lineage" title="Drukpa Lineage">Drukpa Lineage</a>" or "the Land of the Thunder Dragon," a reference to the country's dominant Buddhist sect); "Bhutan" appears only in English-language official correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-lcweb2.loc.gov_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lcweb2.loc.gov-30">[30]</a></sup> The terms for the Kings of Bhutan, <i>Druk Gyalpo</i> ("Dragon King"), and the Bhutanese <a href="/wiki/Endonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Endonym">endonym</a> <i>Drukpa</i>, "Dragon people," are similarly derived.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Names similar to Bhutan—including Bohtan, Buhtan, Bottanthis, Bottan and Bottanter—began to appear in Europe around the 1580s. <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Tavernier" title="Jean-Baptiste Tavernier">Jean-Baptiste Tavernier</a>'s 1676 <i>Six Voyages</i> is the first to record the name <i>Boutan</i>. However, these names seem to have referred not to modern Bhutan but to the <a href="/wiki/Tibet_(1912%E2%80%9351)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tibet (1912–51)">Kingdom of Tibet</a>. The modern distinction between the two did not begin until well into the Scottish explorer <a href="/wiki/George_Bogle_(diplomat)" title="George Bogle (diplomat)">George Bogle</a>'s 1774 expedition. Realizing the differences between the two regions, cultures, and states, his final report to the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> formally proposed calling the <a href="/wiki/Druk_Desi" title="Druk Desi">Druk Desi</a>'s kingdom "Boutan" and the <a href="/wiki/Panchen_Lama" title="Panchen Lama">Panchen Lama</a>'s kingdom "Tibet". The EIC's surveyor general <a href="/wiki/James_Rennell" title="James Rennell">James Rennell</a> first <a href="/wiki/Anglicized" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicized">anglicized</a> the French name as "Bootan," and then popularized the distinction between it and <a href="/wiki/Greater_Tibet" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Tibet">Greater Tibet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuensel_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuensel-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first time a separate Kingdom of Bhutan appeared on a western map, it did so under its local name "Broukpa".<sup id="cite_ref-Kuensel_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuensel-32">[32]</a></sup> Others include <i>Lho Mon</i> ("Dark Southland"), <i>Lho Tsendenjong</i> ("Southland of the <a href="/wiki/Cypress" title="Cypress">Cypress</a>"), <i>Lhomen Khazhi</i> ("Southland of the Four Approaches") and <i>Lho Menjong</i> ("Southland of the <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_herb" class="mw-redirect" title="Medicinal herb">Herbs</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhutan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bhutan" title="History of Bhutan">History of Bhutan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Bhutanese_history" title="Timeline of Bhutanese history">Timeline of Bhutanese history</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia,_Persia,_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif/lossy-page1-220px-A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif/lossy-page1-330px-A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif/lossy-page1-440px-A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia%2C_Persia%2C_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="12779" data-file-height="8512"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:A_map_of_the_countries_between_Constantinople_and_Calcutta_including_Turkey_in_Asia,_Persia,_Afghanistan_and_Turkestan.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bhutan, shaded in green in the center of three Himalayan regions, shown on a 1912 map of Western Asia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Stone tools, weapons, elephants, and remnants of large stone structures provide evidence that Bhutan was inhabited as early as 2000 BC, although there are no existing records from that time. Historians have theorized that the state of <i>Lhomon</i> (literally, "southern darkness"), or <i>Monyul</i> ("Dark Land", a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Monpa_people" title="Monpa people">Monpa</a>, an ethnic group in Bhutan &amp; Arunachal Pradesh) may have existed between 500 BC and AD 600. The names <i>Lhomon Tsendenjong</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">Sandalwood</a> Country), and <i>Lhomon Khashi</i>, or Southern Mon (country of four approaches), have been found in ancient Bhutanese and Tibetan chronicles.<sup id="cite_ref-WIAS_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIAS-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CS0_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS0-36">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cloud-hidden,_whereabouts_unknown_(Paro,_Bhutan).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg/250px-Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg/375px-Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg/500px-Cloud-hidden%2C_whereabouts_unknown_%28Paro%2C_Bhutan%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="532"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cloud-hidden,_whereabouts_unknown_(Paro,_Bhutan).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Dzong_architecture" title="Dzong architecture">dzong</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Paro_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Paro valley">Paro valley</a>, built in 1646.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> was first introduced to Bhutan in the 7th century AD. Tibetan king <a href="/wiki/Songts%C3%A4n_Gampo" class="mw-redirect" title="Songtsän Gampo">Songtsän Gampo</a><sup id="cite_ref-Padel_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padel-37">[37]</a></sup> (reigned 627–649), a convert to Buddhism, who actually had extended the Tibetan Empire into Sikkim and Bhutan,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> ordered the construction of two Buddhist temples, at <a href="/wiki/Bumthang_(town)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bumthang (town)">Bumthang</a> in central Bhutan and at Kyichu (near <a href="/wiki/Paro,_Bhutan" title="Paro, Bhutan">Paro</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Paro_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Paro Valley">Paro Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CS1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS1-39">[39]</a></sup> Buddhism was propagated in earnest<sup id="cite_ref-Padel_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padel-37">[37]</a></sup> in 746<sup id="cite_ref-Hattaway_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hattaway-40">[40]</a></sup> under King Sindhu Rāja (<i>also</i> Künjom;<sup id="cite_ref-Rennie_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rennie-41">[41]</a></sup> Sendha Gyab; Chakhar Gyalpo), an exiled <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indian</a> king who had established a government in Bumthang at Chakhar Gutho Palace.<sup id="cite_ref-HBB_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBB-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>35</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harding_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harding-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>13</span></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg/250px-Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg/375px-Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg/500px-Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tashigang_Dzong_111120.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Trashigang_Dzong" title="Trashigang Dzong">Trashigang Dzong</a>, built in 1659.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Much of early Bhutanese history is unclear because most of the records were destroyed when fire ravaged the ancient capital, <a href="/wiki/Punakha" title="Punakha">Punakha</a>, in 1827. By the 10th century, Bhutan's political development was heavily influenced by its religious history. Various subsects of Buddhism emerged that were patronized by the various <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> warlords. </p>
      <p>Bhutan may have been influenced by the Yuan dynasty with which it shares various cultural and religious similarities. </p>
      <p>After the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> in the 14th century, these subsects vied with each other for supremacy in the political and religious landscape, eventually leading to the ascendancy of the <a href="/wiki/Drukpa_Lineage" title="Drukpa Lineage">Drukpa Lineage</a> by the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-CS1_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS1-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CS4_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS4-44">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wangdue_Zam,_Samuel_Davis,_1783.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg/220px-Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg/330px-Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg/440px-Wangdue_Zam%2C_Samuel_Davis%2C_1783.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1256" data-file-height="706"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wangdue_Zam,_Samuel_Davis,_1783.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Sketch of Bhutan's interior, 1783
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Choka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/View_of_Choka.jpg/220px-View_of_Choka.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/View_of_Choka.jpg/330px-View_of_Choka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/View_of_Choka.jpg/440px-View_of_Choka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2732"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Choka.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Painting of Bhutan, 1813
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck,_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire,_KCIE,_at_Punakha,_Bhutan,_1905.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg/220px-Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg/330px-Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg/440px-Durbar_of_Ugyen_Wangchuck%2C_receiving_the_order_of_the_Knight_Commander_of_the_Indian_Empire%2C_KCIE%2C_at_Punakha%2C_Bhutan%2C_1905.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3998" data-file-height="3314"></a>

         </div>British envoys in Bhutan's royal court in 1905
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Thrikheb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/Thrikheb.jpg/250px-Thrikheb.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f4/Thrikheb.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="366" data-file-height="365"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Thrikheb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <i>thrikhep</i> (throne cover) from the 19th century. Throne covers were placed atop the temple cushions used by high <a href="/wiki/Lama" title="Lama">lamas</a>. The central circular swirling quadrune is the <a href="/wiki/Gankyil" title="Gankyil">gankyil</a> in its mode as the "Four Joys".
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Locally, Bhutan has been known by many names. The earliest Western record of Bhutan, the 1627 <i>Relação</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portuguese</a> <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuits</a> <a href="/wiki/Est%C3%AAv%C3%A3o_Cacella" title="Estêvão Cacella">Estêvão Cacella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Cabral" title="João Cabral">João Cabral</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> records its name variously as <i>Cambirasi</i> (among the <a href="/wiki/Cooch_Behar_State" title="Cooch Behar State">Koch Biharis</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>), <i>Potente</i>, and <i>Mon</i> (an endonym for southern Tibet).<sup id="cite_ref-Kuensel_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuensel-32">[32]</a></sup> Until the early 17th century, Bhutan existed as a patchwork of minor warring <a href="/wiki/Fiefdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiefdom">fiefdoms</a>, when the area was unified by the Tibetan lama and military leader <a href="/wiki/Ngawang_Namgyal" title="Ngawang Namgyal">Ngawang Namgyal</a>, who had fled religious persecution in Tibet. To defend the country against intermittent Tibetan forays, Namgyal built a network of impregnable <i><a href="/wiki/Dzong_architecture" title="Dzong architecture">dzongs</a></i> or fortresses, and <a href="/wiki/Promulgation" title="Promulgation">promulgated</a> the <a href="/wiki/Tsa_Yig" title="Tsa Yig">Tsa Yig</a>, a code of law that helped to bring local lords under centralized control. Many such <i>dzong</i> still exist and are active centers of religion and district administration. <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuits</a> <a href="/wiki/Est%C3%AAv%C3%A3o_Cacella" title="Estêvão Cacella">Estêvão Cacella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Cabral" title="João Cabral">João Cabral</a> were the first recorded Europeans to visit Bhutan in 1627,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> on their way to Tibet. They met Zhabdrung Ngawang Namgyal, presented him with firearms, gunpowder and a telescope, and offered him their services in the war against Tibet, but the Zhabdrung declined the offer. After a stay of nearly eight months Cacella wrote a long letter from the <a href="/wiki/Chagri_Monastery" title="Chagri Monastery">Chagri Monastery</a> reporting on his travels. This is a rare extant report of the Zhabdrung.<sup id="cite_ref-LP_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>When Ngawang Namgyal died in 1651, his passing was kept secret for 54 years. After a period of consolidation, Bhutan lapsed into internal conflict. In 1711 Bhutan went to war against the <a href="/wiki/Raja" title="Raja">Raja</a> of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cooch_Behar_State" title="Cooch Behar State">Koch Bihar</a> in the south. During the chaos that followed, the Tibetans unsuccessfully attacked Bhutan in 1714.<sup id="cite_ref-CS3_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS3-50">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 18th century, the Bhutanese invaded and occupied the kingdom of Koch Bihar. In 1772, the <a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">Maharaja</a> of Koch Bihar appealed to the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> which assisted by ousting the Bhutanese and later in attacking Bhutan itself in 1774. A peace treaty was signed in which Bhutan agreed to retreat to its pre-1730 borders. However, the peace was tenuous, and border skirmishes with the <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British</a> were to continue for the next hundred years. The skirmishes eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Duar_War" title="Duar War">Duar War</a> (1864–65), a confrontation for control of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> <a href="/wiki/Duars" class="mw-redirect" title="Duars">Duars</a>. After Bhutan lost the war, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Sinchula" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Sinchula">Treaty of Sinchula</a> was signed between <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> and Bhutan. As part of the <a href="/wiki/War_reparations" title="War reparations">war reparations</a>, the Duars were ceded to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a> in exchange for a rent of Rs. 50,000. The treaty ended all hostilities between British India and Bhutan. </p>
      <p>During the 1870s, power struggles between the rival valleys of <a href="/wiki/Paro,_Bhutan" title="Paro, Bhutan">Paro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tongsa" class="mw-redirect" title="Tongsa">Tongsa</a> led to civil war in Bhutan, eventually leading to the ascendancy of <a href="/wiki/Ugyen_Wangchuck" title="Ugyen Wangchuck">Ugyen Wangchuck</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Penlop_of_Trongsa" title="Penlop of Trongsa">penlop</a></i> (governor) of <a href="/wiki/Trongsa_District" title="Trongsa District">Trongsa</a>. From his power base in central Bhutan, Ugyen Wangchuck defeated his political enemies and united the country following several civil wars and rebellions during 1882–85.<sup id="cite_ref-CS2_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS2-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1907, an epochal year for the country, Ugyen Wangchuck was unanimously chosen as the hereditary king of the country by the Lhengye Tshog of leading Buddhist monks, government officials, and heads of important families, with the firm petition made by <i>Gongzim</i> <a href="/wiki/Ugyen_Dorji" title="Ugyen Dorji">Ugyen Dorji</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Claude_White" title="John Claude White">John Claude White</a>, British Political Agent in Bhutan, took photographs of the ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The British government promptly recognized the new monarchy, and in 1910 Bhutan signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Punakha" title="Treaty of Punakha">Treaty of Punakha</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Subsidiary_alliance" title="Subsidiary alliance">subsidiary alliance</a> which gave the British control of Bhutan's foreign affairs and meant that Bhutan was treated as an Indian <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely state</a>. This had little real effect, given Bhutan's historical reticence, and also did not appear to affect Bhutan's traditional relations with Tibet. After the new <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_India" title="Dominion of India">Union of India</a> gained <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Independence of India">independence</a> from the United Kingdom on 15 August 1947, Bhutan became one of the first countries to recognize India's independence. On 8 August 1949, a treaty similar to that of 1910, in which Britain had gained power over Bhutan's foreign relations, was signed with the newly independent India.<sup id="cite_ref-WIAS_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIAS-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1953, King <a href="/wiki/Jigme_Dorji_Wangchuck" title="Jigme Dorji Wangchuck">Jigme Dorji Wangchuck</a> established the country's legislature – a 130-member <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly" title="National Assembly">National Assembly</a> – to promote a more democratic form of governance. In 1965, he set up a Royal Advisory Council, and in 1968 he formed a Cabinet. In 1971, Bhutan was admitted to the United Nations, having held observer status for three years. In July 1972, Jigme Singye Wangchuck ascended to the throne at the age of sixteen after the death of his father, Dorji Wangchuck. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Manmohan_Singh_along_with_other_Head_of_State_and_Government_of_SAARC_Countries_witnessed_the_Lha-dhar_Hoisting_(Hoisting_of_Traditional_Large_Flag),_on_the_sidelines_of_the_16th_SAARC_Summit,_in_Thimphu,_Bhutan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Manmohan_Singh_along_with_other_Head_of_State_and_Government_of_SAARC_Countries_witnessed_the_Lha-dhar_Hoisting_%28Hoisting_of_Traditional_Large_Flag%29%2C_on_the_sidelines_of_the_16th_SAARC_Summit%2C_in_Thimphu%2C_Bhutan.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Manmohan_Singh_along_with_other_Head_of_State_and_Government_of_SAARC_Countries_witnessed_the_Lha-dhar_Hoisting_%28Hoisting_of_Traditional_Large_Flag%29%2C_on_the_sidelines_of_the_16th_SAARC_Summit%2C_in_Thimphu%2C_Bhutan.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Manmohan_Singh_along_with_other_Head_of_State_and_Government_of_SAARC_Countries_witnessed_the_Lha-dhar_Hoisting_%28Hoisting_of_Traditional_Large_Flag%29%2C_on_the_sidelines_of_the_16th_SAARC_Summit%2C_in_Thimphu%2C_Bhutan.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1631" data-file-height="1197"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Manmohan_Singh_along_with_other_Head_of_State_and_Government_of_SAARC_Countries_witnessed_the_Lha-dhar_Hoisting_(Hoisting_of_Traditional_Large_Flag),_on_the_sidelines_of_the_16th_SAARC_Summit,_in_Thimphu,_Bhutan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Leaders of Afghanistan, Bhutan, Bangladesh, India, Pakistan, Sri Lanka, Maldives and Nepal at the 16th SAARC Summit in Thimphu, 2010
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bhutan's sixth Five-Year Plan (1987–92) included a policy of 'one nation, one people' and introduced a code of traditional Drukpa dress and etiquette called <i>Driglam Namzhag</i> . The dress element of this code required all citizens to wear the <i>gho</i> (a knee-length robe for men) and the <i>kira</i> (an ankle-length dress for women).<sup id="cite_ref-academic.oup.com_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academic.oup.com-53">[53]</a></sup> A central plank of the Bhutanese government's policy since the late 1960s has been to modernize the use of <a href="/wiki/Dzongkha" title="Dzongkha">Dzongkha</a> language. This began with abandoning the use of <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> in 1964; a language that was adopted to help start formal secular education in the country.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://www.researchgate.net/profile/George-Van-Driem/publication/316659138_Language_policy_in_Bhutan/links/590b01830f7e9b1d0826a372/Language-policy-in-Bhutan.pdf">[1]</a> As a result, at the beginning of the school year in March 1990 the teaching of Nepali language (which share similarities with Hindi) spoken by ethnic <a href="/wiki/Lhotshampas" class="mw-redirect" title="Lhotshampas">Lhotshampas</a> in southern Bhutan was discontinued and all Nepali curricular materials discontinued from Bhutanese schools.<sup id="cite_ref-academic.oup.com_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academic.oup.com-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1988 Bhutan conducted a census in southern Bhutan to guard against illegal immigration, a constant threat in the south where borders with India is porous.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Each family was required to present census workers with a tax receipt from the year 1958—no earlier, no later—or with a certificate of origin, which had to be obtained from one's place of birth, to prove that they were indeed Bhutanese citizens. Previously issued citizenship cards were no longer accepted as proof of citizenship. Alarmed by these measures, many began to protest for civil and cultural rights, and demanded a total change to be brought to the political system that existed since 1907. As protests and related violence swept across southern Bhutan, the government in turn increased its resistance. People present at protests were labeled "anti-national terrorists".<sup id="cite_ref-hdl.handle.net_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle.net-55">[55]</a></sup> After the demonstrations, the Bhutanese army and police began the task of identifying participants and supporters engaged in the violence against the state and people. They were arrested and held for months without trial.<sup id="cite_ref-academic.oup.com_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academic.oup.com-53">[53]</a></sup> Soon the Bhutanese government arbitrarily reported that its census operations had detected the presence in southern Bhutan of over 100,000 "illegal immigrants" although this number is often debated. The census operations, thus, was used as a tool for the identification, eviction and banishment of dissidents who were involved in the uprising against the state. Military and other security forces were deployed for forceful deportations of between 80,000 - 100, 000 <a href="/wiki/Lhotshampas" class="mw-redirect" title="Lhotshampas">Lhotshampas</a> accused of using widespread violence, torture, rape and killing.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The evicted <a href="/wiki/Lhotshampas" class="mw-redirect" title="Lhotshampas">Lhotshampas</a> became refugees in camps in southern <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. Since 2008, many <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western countries</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a>, have allowed resettlement of majority of the <a href="/wiki/Lhotshampa" title="Lhotshampa">Lhotshampa</a> refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-hdl.handle.net_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle.net-55">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_reform_and_modernization">Political reform and modernization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhutan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Political reform and modernization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Bhutan" title="Law of Bhutan">Law of Bhutan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhutanese_democracy" title="Bhutanese democracy">Bhutanese democracy</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Bhutan's political system has recently changed from an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Jigme_Singye_Wangchuck" title="Jigme Singye Wangchuck">Jigme Singye Wangchuck</a> transferred most of his administrative powers to the Council of Cabinet Ministers and allowed for <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachment</a> of the King by a two-thirds majority of the National Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1999, the government lifted a ban on television and internet, making Bhutan one of the last countries to introduce television. In his speech, the King said that television was a critical step to the modernization of Bhutan as well as a major contributor to the country's <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_happiness" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross national happiness">gross national happiness</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-GNH_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GNH-60">[60]</a></sup> but warned that the "misuse" of this new technology could erode traditional Bhutanese values.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A new constitution was presented in early 2005. In December 2005, Wangchuck announced that he would abdicate the throne in his son's favour in 2008. On 14 December 2006, he announced that he would be abdicating immediately. This was followed by the first national <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Bhutan" title="Elections in Bhutan">parliamentary elections</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhutanese_National_Council_election,_2007%E2%80%932008" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhutanese National Council election, 2007–2008">December 2007</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008_Bhutanese_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2008 Bhutanese general election">March 2008</a>. </p>
      <p>On 6 November 2008, 28-year-old <a href="/wiki/Jigme_Khesar_Namgyel_Wangchuck" title="Jigme Khesar Namgyel Wangchuck">Jigme Khesar Namgyel Wangchuck</a> was crowned king.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhutan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bhutan is on the southern slopes of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, landlocked between the <a href="/wiki/Tibet_Autonomous_Region" title="Tibet Autonomous Region">Tibet Autonomous Region</a> of China to the north and the Indian states of <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> to west and south and the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Arunachal_Pradesh" title="Arunachal Pradesh">Arunachal Pradesh</a> to the east. It lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/26th_parallel_north" title="26th parallel north">26°N</a> and <a href="/wiki/29th_parallel_north" title="29th parallel north">29°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/88th_meridian_east" title="88th meridian east">88°E</a> and <a href="/wiki/93rd_meridian_east" title="93rd meridian east">93°E</a>. The land consists mostly of steep and high <a href="/wiki/Mountains_of_Bhutan" title="Mountains of Bhutan">mountains</a> crisscrossed by a network of swift rivers that form deep valleys before draining into the Indian plains. Elevation rises from 200&nbsp;m (660&nbsp;ft) in the southern foothills to more than 7,000&nbsp;m (23,000&nbsp;ft). This great geographical diversity combined with equally diverse climate conditions contributes to Bhutan's outstanding range of biodiversity and ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-official_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg/220px-Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg/330px-Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg/440px-Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2136"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tashich%C3%B6dzong_Thimphu-2008-01-23.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Snowfall in Bhutan
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bhutan's northern region consists of an arc of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Himalayan_alpine_shrub_and_meadows" title="Eastern Himalayan alpine shrub and meadows">Eastern Himalayan alpine shrub and meadows</a> reaching up to glaciated mountain peaks with an extremely cold climate at the highest elevations. Most peaks in the north are over 7,000&nbsp;m (23,000&nbsp;ft) above sea level; the highest point is 7,570-meter (24,840&nbsp;ft)-tall <a href="/wiki/Gangkhar_Puensum" title="Gangkhar Puensum">Gangkhar Puensum</a>, which has the distinction of being the <a href="/wiki/Highest_unclimbed_mountain" title="Highest unclimbed mountain">highest unclimbed mountain</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-63">[63]</a></sup> The lowest point, at 98&nbsp;m (322&nbsp;ft), is in the valley of <a href="/wiki/Drangme_Chhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Drangme Chhu">Drangme Chhu</a>, where the river crosses the border with India.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-63">[63]</a></sup> Watered by snow-fed rivers, alpine valleys in this region provide pasture for livestock, tended by a sparse population of migratory shepherds. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Mountains_(Bhutan)" title="Black Mountains (Bhutan)">Black Mountains</a> in Bhutan's central region form a watershed between two major river systems: the <a href="/wiki/Mo_Chhu" title="Mo Chhu">Mo Chhu</a> and the Drangme Chhu. Peaks in the Black Mountains range between 1,500 and 4,925&nbsp;m (4,921 and 16,158&nbsp;ft) above sea level, and fast-flowing rivers have carved out deep gorges in the lower mountain areas. The forests of the central Bhutan mountains consist of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Himalayan_subalpine_conifer_forests" title="Eastern Himalayan subalpine conifer forests">Eastern Himalayan subalpine conifer forests</a> in higher elevations and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Himalayan_broadleaf_forests" title="Eastern Himalayan broadleaf forests">Eastern Himalayan broadleaf forests</a> in lower elevations. Woodlands of the central region provide most of Bhutan's forest production. The <a href="/wiki/Torsa_River" title="Torsa River">Torsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raidak" class="mw-redirect" title="Raidak">Raidak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sankosh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sankosh">Sankosh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manas_River" title="Manas River">Manas</a> are Bhutan's main rivers, flowing through this region. Most of the population lives in the central highlands. </p>
      <p>In the south, the <a href="/wiki/Shiwalik_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Shiwalik Hills">Shiwalik Hills</a> are covered with dense <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_subtropical_broadleaf_forests" title="Himalayan subtropical broadleaf forests">Himalayan subtropical broadleaf forests</a>, alluvial lowland river valleys, and mountains up to around 1,500&nbsp;m (4,900&nbsp;ft) above sea level. The foothills descend into the subtropical <a href="/wiki/Duars" class="mw-redirect" title="Duars">Duars</a> Plain, which is the eponymous gateway to strategic mountain passes (also known as dwars or dooars; literally, "doors" in <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maithili_language" title="Maithili language">Maithili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri_language" title="Bhojpuri language">Bhojpuri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magahi_language" title="Magahi language">Magahi</a> languages.).<sup id="cite_ref-Bhutan_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhutan-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Most of the Duars is in India, but a 10 to 15&nbsp;km (6.2 to 9.3&nbsp;mi)-wide strip extends into Bhutan. The Bhutan Duars is divided into two parts, the northern and southern Duars </p>
      <p>The northern Duars, which abut the Himalayan foothills, have rugged, sloping terrain and dry, porous soil with dense vegetation and abundant wildlife. The southern Duars has moderately fertile soil, heavy <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> grass, dense, mixed jungle, and freshwater springs. Mountain rivers, fed by melting snow or monsoon rains, empty into the <a href="/wiki/Brahmaputra_River" title="Brahmaputra River">Brahmaputra River</a> in India. Data released by the Ministry of Agriculture showed that the country had a forest cover of 64% as of October 2005. </p>
